{"uri": "2024-08-442571109", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-03", "time": "06:10:17", "dateTime": "2024-08-03T06:10:17Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-03T06:00:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8235294222831726, "url": "https://www.irishtimes.com/sport/2024/08/03/rhys-mcclenaghan-perfection-isnt-attainable-but-well-try/", "title": "Rhys McClenaghan: 'Perfection isn't attainable - but we'll try'", "body": "The number-one ranked gymnast just wants to 'land on top of the podium'\n\nThere is a website available in Paris for the exclusive use of accredited media and the army of team officials that support the athletes called MyInfo. In the biographies for the gymnasts, who compete in Saturday's men's pommel final, Rhys McClenaghan is asked about ambitions. His answer is one line.\n\n\"Landing on top of the podium at the Paris 2024 Olympic Games.\"\n\nThe answer could have been taken from Tokyo 2020. On Saturday night, in the Bercy Arena, McClenaghan is chasing an ambition that was alive three years ago and perished on one mistake in the final.\n\nA missed hand position and fall pushed him down the field into seventh place. He left Tokyo chastened but also stoic and determined. The Olympic Games, the damned Olympics, had struck again.\n\nBecause of Covid it has been a three-year wait, not four, for the Olympic cycle to continue. McClenaghan has thrillingly pushed on and in 2022 became world champion in Liverpool. A year later he successfully defended his world title in Antwerp.\n\nIn October of that year he told The Irish Times: \"Many athletes would agree it's probably more difficult to retain a title than it is to get it in the first place. Because you've got a target on your back.\"\n\nWell, he didn't win an Olympic medal in Tokyo but as a two-time World Champion coming into Paris, McClenaghan is the man to beat.\n\nAfter confidently qualifying a week ago for the eight-man final, McClenaghan is ranked number one with his score of 15.200. He seems acutely aware of his place in the gymnastic firmament, conscious enough at least to state in public where he is in his own head about the prospects of fulfilling the ambition that failure in Tokyo has sharpened.\n\n\"I'm at the top of the mountain now, and I'm just enjoying the view,\" the 25 year old said after qualifying for the final. \"It could be better, but it was solid. That was the word Luke [Carson, his coach] was using when I came off. It was calm. That's what you want to be like in that reappearance at the Olympic Games, and that's what it did feel like.\n\n\"But of course, I want to be pushing more and more to that perfection that isn't attainable. But we'll try.\"\n\nEven after his impressive qualification at the top of a 65-strong field, McClenaghan and Carson had not decided what routine they would use in the final. Those in the know say the pommel closely relates to the balance beam in women's gymnastics because there remains a fearful sense the athletes might not make it through to the end of the routine - as McClenaghan painfully demonstrated three years ago before he was 10 seconds in. Completion is the first rung on the ladder of success.\n\nIn essence it is about mental and physical agility, where muscle memory is key. It is also a truism of the sport that if the athlete tries to memorise the moves in sequence, he is toast before he vaults on to the pommel. It needs to be ingrained.\n\nThere is also the endurance, rhythm and precision required to not touch the apparatus as the legs \"flare\" from side to side over the 45 seconds or so. The scoring comes down to execution and difficulty with the athlete deciding before he competes on how far he wants to push the difficulty element of the routine.\n\nIt is a risk/reward settlement McClenaghan will agree with himself and his team as well as an expression of self-belief. To set the routine at a dizzy Olympic gold medal-winning level of difficulty is not within the ability of every athlete. For McClenaghan it is. Saturday's final performance will not be the same as the one that got him to the Bercy Arena.\n\n\"It is different,\" said McClenaghan last week on his planned final routine. \"I can upgrade and I plan to upgrade. Hopefully we'll be seeing that score bumped up even further. It felt so familiar out there, that pressure I have on my shoulders, because I do it every day in training. I don't know what score it will take to win, and I don't care, as I have my job to do and I am hopefully going to do it.\"\n\nIt would be remiss to believe he has a straight run to the podium. In Tokyo the title went to an opponent he knows well, Great Britain's Max Whitlock, a three-time Olympic champion, who has won the Olympic title once in the floor routine and twice on the pommel. In Tokyo Whitlock became the first man in more than 40 years to defend a pommel horse gold medal.\n\nReversing his decision to retire after Tokyo, he announced Paris would be his fourth and final Games. And Whitlock duly made his presence felt in qualifying as one of five athletes to score over 15 points.\n\nMcClenaghan knows his way around the game of margins as well as the personalities of Whitlock and the other top qualifiers, Japan's Takaaki Sugino (15.033), Ukraine's Oleg Verniaiev (15.033), Kazakhstan's Nariman Kurbanov (15.000), Korea's Woong Hur (14.900), and Dutch gymnast Loran de Munck (14.760).\n\nHe is where he has always wanted to be, from when he travelled to London 12 years ago to watch the pommel competition as a teenage fan. He told the BBC about how he deeply admired how British silver medal winner Louis Smith performed under the weight of his home crowd.\n\n\"I saw the amount of pressure that was on that man's shoulders and to see him presenting to the judges and performing a routine like he always did in training, that for me was so admirable,\" he told Nigel Ringland. \"It's a little moment that I'll never forget, that inspired me until this point. My whole career is filled with moments like that, moments of surreal, dreamlike fantasies that I've had growing up, to be competing against Louis.\"\n\nOn Saturday night, there are no fantasies. It's just McClenaghan and the weight of the expectations he has heaped on himself. Already a two-time world champion and three-time European champion, he is regarded as one of the greats. But it is still not enough for McClenaghan. That wasn't in the script as the two-time pommel horse gold medal winner made his Olympic farewell.\n\nThen, in any gymnast's biggest fear, Loran de Munck made a mistake right before McClenaghan's run, something he witnessed unfold as he ran his routine through his mind.\n\nThree years ago in Tokyo, it was McClenaghan who made a mistake when it mattered most as his medal chance slipped away.\n\nIn Paris on Saturday, those ghosts were well and truly banished.\n\nMcClenaghan produced a flawless routine. His coach, and friend, Luke Carson watched on and had his head in his hands just before McClenaghan dismounted.\n\nHe knew what was about to come if it was a clean landing - and it was.\n\nThe arena erupted and McClenaghan let out a huge roar before hugging Carson, his coach since he was 14.\n\nHe admits the biggest emotion was \"relief\" after completing \"the most difficult routine I could do\".\n\n\"That's where the tears and emotions came from, really.\n\n\"Relief is always the one when you land the dismount. But his flawless performance here reinforced all he achieved before, including winning back-to-back world titles.\n\nSuch is the Zen and the art of being Rhys McClenaghan.\n\nNariman Kurbanov from Kazakhstan was up first, the 26-year-old scoring 15.434 and laying down an early marker with his slowly soaring routine which clearly impressed the judges. It would be good enough to win him silver.\n\nThen came Max Whitlock, the 31-year-old British gymnast producing an amazing handstand mid routine, yet his score of 15.000 appeared to slightly disappoint. He would finish in fourth. Whitlock was seeking to become the first gymnast to win three pommel horse Olympic gold medals in a row, adding to those won in Rio and Tokyo.\n\nUp after McClenaghan was Stephen Nedoroscik, the 25-year-old from the US. The 2021 world champion's one-handed twists mid routine clearly impressed the judges too, but his score of 15.300 was not enough to trouble McClenaghan and it left him in third.\n\nSo McClenaghan also extends Ireland's Olympic medal record into a seventh sport - joining those won in athletics, boxing, swimming, sailing, rowing and equestrian.\n\nThe 25-year-old from Newtownards has only been growing in confidence since his Olympic quest fell short in Tokyo three years ago. All that was on display here with his routine perfectly executed under the circumstances.\n\nHe was back in the arena exactly seven days after confidently qualifying for the eight-man final ranked number one with his score of 15.200.\n\nSo far in Paris, Kellie Harrington is already guaranteed a bronze in lightweight boxing, and will look to upgrade that to silver or gold in her semi-final later on Saturday night.\n\nIn swimming, Daniel Wiffen has already won gold in the 800m freestyle and will go into the 1,500m final as overwhelming favourite. Mona McSharry took bronze in the 100m breaststroke, while rowers Philip Doyle and Daire Lynch won bronze in the men's doubles before Paul O'Donovan and Fintan McCarthy won their second gold in the lightweight double sculls.\n\nIreland's best Olympic medal record was the six won in the London in 2012, which has now been matched, with more to come, as early as Sunday and Wiffen's 1,500m final.\n\nLast Sunday week, McClenaghan celebrated his 25th birthday inside the Athletes' Village, saying he was already living his dream\n\nHe fully understands was this moment means in other ways too. His hopes of making the Olympic podium in Tokyo ended after 10 seconds when he fell chest-first on to the horse and ended up seventh.\n\n\"I'll be walking away from this a more dangerous man,\" he said in the aftermath of that moment. \"Because with disappointment comes an incredible amount of motivation and inspiration.\n\nSo, seven years ago after announcing his arrival at age 18, beating Whitlock to win gold at the 2018 Commonwealth Games, he adds to his eight championship medals in all (two world championship gold and one bronze, three European gold, plus Commonwealth Games gold and silver).\n\nIn the final event immediately before, Simone Biles captured another gold medal, this time in the women's vault final. It was her third gold medal of these Games after leading the USA to gold in the team final and winning gold in the all-around final.\n\nFor her career, it's Biles's seventh gold and 10th medal overall, extending her record of most Olympic medals by an American gymnast. Biles also won gold on vault at the 2016 Rio Olympics.\n\nBut there can only ever be one first Olympic medal winner in gymnastics for Ireland. {"uri": "8258334394", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-04", "time": "07:57:03", "dateTime": "2024-08-04T07:57:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-04T07:55:56Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7607843279838562, "url": "https://www.irishnews.com/news/northern-ireland/joy-in-co-down-as-rhys-mcclenaghan-wins-irelands-first-gymnastics-olympic-gold-WBA7VFVSZBNZRJ4BKGNUZCE57I/", "title": "Joy in Co Down as Rhys McClenaghan wins Ireland's first gymnastics Olympic gold", "body": "A crowd of supporters gathered at Origin Gymnastics in Newtownards screamed and celebrated as gold in the pommel horse was confirmed.\n\nThere was jubilation in Co Down after Rhys McClenaghan clinched Ireland's first ever Olympic gold medal in gymnastics.\n\nAs McClenaghan, 25, took to the pommel horse at the Bercy Arena in Paris, back in his home town of Newtownards a crowd of fans waited with bated breath until his perfectly executed dismount.\n\nThere were cheers at Origin Gymnastics, where McClenaghan trains, but more nerves as the remainder of the gymnasts in the event final completed their routines and the scores were complete.\n\nOrigin senior coach Conor McGovern confirmed the gold to cheers of approval from those gathered waving banners of support.\n\nMcClenaghan told RTE that it felt like a dream, adding: \"But it's a dream well earned and I just can't believe it happened on this day.\n\n\"I always felt like it was going to happen but I wasn't sure when.\n\n\"Listen, everyone at home: see this as an example of, find a dream that you love, chase it and enjoy that journey, because I can tell you, if I fell on my bum today, I still would have loved this journey, every second of it.\n\n\"But I didn't, and today I'm walking away with a gold medal.\n\n\"I knew that this would be the toughest pommel horse final of all time, and it was. So to come out on top is incredible.\n\n\"If this even just inspires someone to walk through the doors of a gymnastics club then I'll be happy.\"\n\nMcClenaghan is the fourth person from Northern Ireland to win a gold medal of the Paris games, following swimmers Daniel Wiffen and Jack McMillian and rower Hannah Scott.\n\nMeanwhile political leaders took to social media to celebrate.\n\nStormont First Minister Michelle O'Neill posted on X: \"Rhys McClenaghan makes history as the first ever gymnast from our island to win gold at the Olympics!\n\n\"What an incredible career that is going from strength to strength, congratulations Rhys!\"\n\nDeputy First Minister Emma Little-Pengelly also congratulated McClenaghan.\n\nIn a joint statement, the two said: \"Rhys' skill, strength and poise is justly rewarded with a gold medal.\n\n\"It is a truly deserved honour for the drive and dedication he has shown throughout his career so far.\"\n\nTaoiseach Simon Harris said in a statement: \"A massive congratulations to Rhys McClenaghan on becoming an Olympic champion.\n\n\"Our hearts pumped while watching your mesmerising performance.\n\n\"Your determination and precision have been top class and you are so deserving of this gold medal.\n\n\"Your journey to this Olympic medal has been nothing short of incredible.\n\n\"We are all so proud of you. I know having met you in Paris last week how much this means to you and your family.\n\n\"Congratulations and enjoy the celebrations.\"\n\nBack at Origin Gymnastics, Mr McGovern said everyone believed McClenaghan could do it.\n\n\"I put my money where my mouth is with a small bet,\" he told the PA news agency.\n\n\"100% everyone here believed in Rhys. It's not to say we knew for a fact, no-one knows, but we had the highest belief in him.\n\n\"Hosting this event was so worth it, the smiles on everyone's faces, so glad.\n\n\"It's not a surprise, him being a double world champion. He's well on his way to becoming one of the best pommel workers of all time.\"\n\nMr McGovern said they had had a rush of interest in joining the club this week.\n\n\"The amount of people even looking in the windows as they walk past, kids coming in, even interactions I have had walking to the cafe, people wishing Rhys good luck. That just means so much,\" he said.\n\n\"Rhys has built a community. There are a lot of people here today, and I think Rhys has had a conversation with every one of them at some stage. His sense of community is second to none.\n\n\"We want this to be a community that is unlike anything else, and I think that we are well on our way to doing that. {"uri": "8257852327", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-03", "time": "20:33:20", "dateTime": "2024-08-03T20:33:20Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-03T20:32:23Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7568627595901489, "url": "https://www.indy100.com/sport/joy-in-co-down-as-rhys-mcclenaghan-wins-irelands-first-gymnastics-olympic-gold", "title": "Joy in Co Down as Rhys McClenaghan wins Ireland's first gymnastics Olympic gold", "body": "Supporters of gymnast Rhys McClenaghan cheer him on at Origin Gym in Newtownards, where he trains (Rebecca Black/PA)\n\nThere was jubilation in Co Down after Rhys McClenaghan clinched Ireland's first ever Olympic gold medal in gymnastics.\n\nAs McClenaghan, 25, took to the pommel horse at the Bercy Arena in Paris, back in his home town of Newtownards a crowd of fans waited with bated breath until his perfectly executed dismount.\n\nThere were cheers at Origin Gymnastics, where McClenaghan trains, but more nerves as the remainder of the gymnasts in the event final completed their routines and the scores were complete.\n\nOrigin senior coach Conor McGovern confirmed the gold to cheers of approval from those gathered waving banners of support.\n\nMcClenaghan told RTE that it felt like a dream, adding: \"But it's a dream well earned and I just can't believe it happened on this day.\n\n\"I always felt like it was going to happen but I wasn't sure when.\n\n\"Listen, everyone at home: see this as an example of, find a dream that you love, chase it and enjoy that journey, because I can tell you, if I fell on my bum today, I still would have loved this journey, every second of it.\n\n\"But I didn't, and today I'm walking away with a gold medal.\n\n\"I knew that this would be the toughest pommel horse final of all time, and it was. So to come out on top is incredible.\n\n\"If this even just inspires someone to walk through the doors of a gymnastics club then I'll be happy.\"\n\nMcClenaghan won Northern Ireland's fourth gold medal of the Paris games, following swimmers Daniel Wiffen and Jack McMillian and rower Hannah Scott.\n\nMeanwhile political leaders took to social media to celebrate.\n\nStormont First Minister Michelle O'Neill posted on X: \"Rhys McClenaghan makes history as the first ever gymnast from our island to win gold at the Olympics!\n\n\"What an incredible career that is going from strength to strength, congratulations Rhys!\"\n\nDeputy First Minister Emma Little-Pengelly also congratulated McClenaghan.\n\nIn a joint statement, the two said: \"Rhys' skill, strength and poise is justly rewarded with a gold medal.\n\n\"It is a truly deserved honour for the drive and dedication he has shown throughout his career so far.\"\n\nTaoiseach Simon Harris said in a statement: \"A massive congratulations to Rhys McClenaghan on becoming an Olympic champion.\n\n\"Our hearts pumped while watching your mesmerising performance.\n\n\"Your determination and precision have been top class and you are so deserving of this gold medal.\n\n\"Your journey to this Olympic medal has been nothing short of incredible.\n\n\"We are all so proud of you. I know having met you in Paris last week how much this means to you and your family.\n\n\"Congratulations and enjoy the celebrations.\"\n\nBack at Origin Gymnastics, Mr McGovern said everyone believed McClenaghan could do it.\n\n\"I put my money where my mouth is with a small bet,\" he told the PA news agency.\n\n\"100% everyone here believed in Rhys. It's not to say we knew for a fact, no-one knows, but we had the highest belief in him.\n\n\"Hosting this event was so worth it, the smiles on everyone's faces, so glad.\n\n\"It's not a surprise, him being a double world champion. He's well on his way to becoming one of the best pommel workers of all time.\"\n\nMr McGovern said they had had a rush of interest in joining the club this week.\n\n\"The amount of people even looking in the windows as they walk past, kids coming in, even interactions I have had walking to the cafe, people wishing Rhys good luck. That just means so much,\" he said.\n\n\"Rhys has built a community. There are a lot of people here today, and I think Rhys has had a conversation with every one of them at some stage. His sense of community is second to none.\n\n\"We want this to be a community that is unlike anything else, and I think that we are well on our way to doing that. That plan developed and came to fruition today.\"\n\nIt was also a case of redemption for McClenaghan, who went into the Olympics in Tokyo three years ago with designs on at least finishing on the podium if not claiming gold then, but a mistake during his routine meant he finished a disappointing seventh.\n\nOn this occasion, however, there would be no denying him. {"uri": "8257737754", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-03", "time": "18:14:00", "dateTime": "2024-08-03T18:14:00Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-03T18:12:25Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7333333492279053, "url": "https://www.the42.ie/rhys-mcclenaghan-18-6454466-Aug2024/", "title": "McClenaghan delivers what he promised on a magical night for Irish sport", "body": "Rhys McClenaghan is an Olympic champion, conquering his Tokyo demons with audacity. Gavin Cooney reports from the Bercy Arena\n\nBENEATH THE CHALK-HAZY lights of the Bercy Arena, Loran de Munck planted his hand a fraction of an inch to the right of where it was meant to go, and so he slipped of the pommel horse and ended his own dreams.\n\nAnother nerve may have been knotted into Rhys McClenaghan.\n\nDe Munck had to remount to finish his routine, which meant finishing later than planned.\n\nMcClenaghan's pre-competition routine is always the same: he turns his back on the competitor immediately before him and instead visualises his routine.\n\nDe Munck wasn't going to medal but now he was meddling. McClenaghan had finished his visualisation and had turned to make his way to the stage.\n\nThe pommel horse final is purely about executing that which you practice to abnormal levels of precision. McClenaghan suddenly had the very first part of it thrown askew.\n\nDe Munck slunk away and McClenaghan was called forward. He slapped his hands on each of the pommels as his coach Luke Carson adjusted them to his height. He then stood adjacent and waited for his call over the arena PA system. Does it always take this long?\n\nGiven McClenaghan was announced as the reigning world champion, any neutral observers would have wondered why everyone back in Ireland was a fretting, tissue-frittering mess.\n\nBut that's because of Tokyo and the awesome weight freighted upon the Olympic Games. It's a cruel kind of caprice, but the rest of the world only truly pays attention during the Olympics. It comes around every four years and so it means it so much more. Too much, if we're honest.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nNariman Kurbanov of Kazakhstan went first and sent an early ripple across the arena, awarded a 15.433, which outstripped McClenaghan's qualifying score. Gulp.\n\nNext was two-time champion Max Whitlock, in his final appearance before retirement. Don't go doing something stupid like writing a perfect farewell for yourself now, Max. But Whitlock erred in means not truly accessible to the vast majority of us. A tiny adjustment before his dismount hurt him, as did the separation of his legs during his swing on the horse.\n\nOne of the iron laws of pommel horse: do not split your legs.\n\n\"Whenever you see a pommel horse worker splitting their legs, they're nervous\", explained McClenaghan after it was all over. \"They've felt something is off a little bit.\"\n\nIn training the routine earlier in the week, McClenaghan split his legs. Do it again and the Olympic gold would be going to somebody else.\n\nHe and his coach Luke Carson believed the routine they had been training could score 15.6. Do that and nobody in the field would live with him. Not Kurbanov, not Nedoroscik, not Whitlock, nor anybody else. McClenaghan is so good it's only about him and it's never about anyone else. How terrifying is that?\n\nThe key to that score is to make it damn difficult. Routines are scored by difficulty and by execution, and in the most elite field in gymnastics, everyone can execute. The greatest separate themselves by the scale of their ambition.\n\nThink for a moment on the sheer sporting audacity of this. McClenaghan was returning to the stage most likely to summon the ghosts of his fall off the horse in the Olympic final three years ago, and he was returning to banish them with a more difficult routine. The most difficult routine of his life.\n\nWhat does that say about you Rhys?\n\n\"It shows I've got balls.\"\n\nAnd so McClenaghan snapped up and atop the pommel to fill the next minute with every single inch of the life he led to that moment. He clutched his legs together and swung great, generous circles which resembled steady radar pulses to the thousands looking down upon him.\n\nHis hands then left the pommel and McClenaghan pat-pat-patted his way along the horse, continuing to swing in wide arcs while also somehow managing to rock forward and back.\n\nHis routine flowed at a brisk and mildly scary tempo, further separating him from his more staccato rivals The crowd's murmurs were evolving to shouts and cheers: they may not know the intricacies of the apparatus but they knew this looked good.\n\nBut still McClenaghan believed he needed more. Another ingredient of difficulty, another hostage to fortune, another chance for it all to go wrong. The added move is known as the Roth, named after the American gymnast, Bill Roth. It's effectively a full journey down the pommel horse while completing a trio of spins.\n\nIt's not the most difficult move in isolation, but to needlessly cram it in to the end of the biggest routine of your life as your muscles scream for sweet relief? That's the stuff that peels the best away from the best.\n\nMcClenaghan knew he might not need to do it. He could just junk it on the fly and finish safely without disaster, without a repeat of Tokyo. But that wouldn't guarantee the gold medal. The best must be audacious.\n\nAnd so McClenaghan completed his final journey down the horse flawlessly, before swinging his legs upwards to complete the handstand that comes before the dismount. The split second it took for his feet to point directly at the sky was long enough for everyone in the arena to realise that Rhys McClenaghan had run his gauntlet and come through without a scratch.\n\nHis feet then hit the floor and the pressure and the tension of the last three years burst forth and set sail.\n\nIt was at that point the rest of us knew.\n\nRhys McClenaghan had done It was the highest ever score he achieved in competition.\n\nMcClenaghan shed tears when the score flashed up on the screen and the crowd went wild. Everybody knew that even though four gymnasts still had to complete their routine, McClenaghan was the Olympic champion.\n\nIt was his seventh gold medal including World, European and Commonwealth titles but this was by far the most significant, the one he wanted from the moment he committed himself to the sport in his teenage years.\n\nNariman Kurbanov from Kazakhstan who was the first finalist to tackle the pommel horse set an extraordinary high standard with a score of 15.434 consisting of 6.7 for difficult and 8.7 for execution.\n\nIn his final ever Pommel horse event, Whitlock's bid for gold ended with a score of 15.3.\n\nThe Netherlands' Loran de Munck fell off the horse which ended his ambitions. McClenaghan was next and after a short break American Stephen Nedoroscik was the only serious threat remaining.\n\nHe did exceptionally well with a score of 15.33 which it didn't dislodge the Irishman though it was good enough to earn him the bronze medal with Kurbanov taking the silver.\n\nAfter his nightmare in Tokyo three years this was redemption for McClenaghan. His life's ambition achieved at the age of 25.\n\nSpeaking afterwards a beaming McClenaghan reflected on the enormity of the achievement: \"It feels like a dream, it's a dream well-earned and I just can't believe it's happened! It always felt like it was going to happen but I wasn't sure when.\n\n\"Listen everybody at home see this as an example of find a dream that you love, chase it and enjoy that journey because I can tell you if I fell off that Pommel today I still would have loved this journey, every single second of it but I didn't and today I'm walking away with an Olympic gold medal!\"\n\n\"After the routine I was happy - I wasn't thinking about medals. When the score came up I was proud of that score because it was mine and I wasn't thinking of the podium at all I was just thinking I've done my job and I feel like that's where the main emotions came from today.\n\n\"I knew that this would also be the toughest pommel horse final of all time, and it was so to come out on top is incredible.\"", "source": {"uri": "independent.ie", "dataType": "news", {"uri": "8257757052", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-03", "time": "18:34:56", "dateTime": "2024-08-03T18:34:56Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-03T18:34:13Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7215686440467834, "url": "https://www.thejournal.ie/rhys-mcclenaghan-18-2-6454666-Aug2024/", "title": "McClenaghan delivers what he promised on a magical night for Irish sport", "body": "Rhys McClenaghan is an Olympic champion, conquering his Tokyo demons with audacity. Gavin Cooney reports from the Bercy Arena\n\nThe 42 Editor's note: This article by Gavin Cooney forms part of The 42's subscriber-only coverage of the Paris 2024 Olympics, with unique insights and fresh perspectives on the biggest stories every single day. If you'd enjoy more great sportswriting like this, you can sign up here for a free one-month trial to The 42.\n\nBENEATH THE CHALK-HAZY lights of the Bercy Arena, Loran de Munck planted his hand a fraction of an inch to the right of where it was meant to go, and so he slipped of the pommel horse and ended his own dreams.\n\nAnother nerve may have been knotted into Rhys McClenaghan.\n\nDe Munck had to remount to finish his routine, which meant finishing later than planned.\n\nMcClenaghan's pre-competition routine is always the same: he turns his back on the competitor immediately before him and instead visualises his routine.\n\nDe Munck wasn't going to medal but now he was meddling. McClenaghan had finished his visualisation and had turned to make his way to the stage.\n\nThe pommel horse final is purely about executing that which you practice to abnormal levels of precision. McClenaghan suddenly had the very first part of it thrown askew.\n\nDe Munck slunk away and McClenaghan was called forward. He slapped his hands on each of the pommels as his coach Luke Carson adjusted them to his height. He then stood adjacent and waited for his call over the arena PA system. Does it always take this long?\n\nGiven McClenaghan was announced as the reigning world champion, any neutral observers would have wondered why everyone back in Ireland was a fretting, tissue-frittering mess.\n\nBut that's because of Tokyo and the awesome weight freighted upon the Olympic Games. It's a cruel kind of caprice, but the rest of the world only truly pays attention during the Olympics. It comes around every four years and so it means it so much more. Too much, if we're honest.\n\nAdvertisement\n\nNariman Kurbanov of Kazakhstan went first and sent an early ripple across the arena, awarded a 15.433, which outstripped McClenaghan's qualifying score. Gulp.\n\nNext was two-time champion Max Whitlock, in his final appearance before retirement. Don't go doing something stupid like writing a perfect farewell for yourself now, Max. But Whitlock erred in means not truly accessible to the vast majority of us. A tiny adjustment before his dismount hurt him, as did the separation of his legs during his swing on the horse.\n\nOne of the iron laws of pommel horse: do not split your legs.\n\n\"Whenever you see a pommel horse worker splitting their legs, they're nervous\", explained McClenaghan after it was all over. \"They've felt something is off a little bit.\"\n\nIn training the routine earlier in the week, McClenaghan split his legs. Do it again and the Olympic gold would be going to somebody else.\n\nHe and his coach Luke Carson believed the routine they had been training could score 15.6. Do that and nobody in the field would live with him. Not Kurbanov, not Nedoroscik, not Whitlock, nor anybody else. McClenaghan is so good it's only about him and it's never about anyone else. How terrifying is that?\n\nThe key to that score is to make it damn difficult. Routines are scored by difficulty and by execution, and in the most elite field in gymnastics, everyone can execute. The greatest separate themselves by the scale of their ambition.\n\nThink for a moment on the sheer sporting audacity of this. McClenaghan was returning to the stage most likely to summon the ghosts of his fall off the horse in the Olympic final three years ago, and he was returning to banish them with a more difficult routine. The most difficult routine of his life.\n\nWhat does that say about you Rhys?\n\n\"It shows I've got balls.\"\n\nAnd so McClenaghan snapped up and atop the pommel to fill the next minute with every single inch of the life he led to that moment. He clutched his legs together and swung great, generous circles which resembled steady radar pulses to the thousands looking down upon him.\n\nHis hands then left the pommel and McClenaghan pat-pat-patted his way along the horse, continuing to swing in wide arcs while also somehow managing to rock forward and back.\n\nRead Next Related Reads An overnight success years in the making - How Irish swimming became an Olympic force 'It's hands on wall first': The most important 0.01 seconds of Mona McSharry's career\n\nHis routine flowed at a brisk and mildly scary tempo, further separating him from his more staccato rivals. The crowd's murmurs were evolving to shouts and cheers: they may not know the intricacies of the apparatus but they knew this looked good.\n\nBut still McClenaghan believed he needed more. Another ingredient of difficulty, another hostage to fortune, another chance for it all to go wrong. The added move is known as the Roth, named after the American gymnast, Bill Roth. It's effectively a full journey down the pommel horse while completing a trio of spins.\n\nIt's not the most difficult move in isolation, but to needlessly cram it in to the end of the biggest routine of your life as your muscles scream for sweet relief? That's the stuff that peels the best away from the best.\n\nMcClenaghan knew he might not need to do it. He could just junk it on the fly and finish safely without disaster, without a repeat of Tokyo. But that wouldn't guarantee the gold medal. The best must be audacious.\n\nAnd so McClenaghan completed his final journey down the horse flawlessly, before swinging his legs upwards to complete the handstand that comes before the dismount. The split second it took for his feet to point directly at the sky was long enough for everyone in the arena to realise that Rhys McClenaghan had run his gauntlet and come through without a scratch.\n\nHis feet then hit the floor and the pressure and the tension of the last three years burst forth and set sail.\n\nIt was at that point the rest of us knew.\n\nRhys McClenaghan had done what he had come to do.\n\nWritten by Gavin Cooney and originally published on The 42 whose award-winning team produces original content that you won't find anywhere else: on GAA, League of Ireland, women's sport and boxing, as well as our game-changing rugby coverage, all with an Irish eye. Full list for day 8 in Paris\n\nREAD MORE: What time is gymnast Rhys McClenaghan going for Olympic gold today?\n\nThroughout his life, he has projected his fantasies towards today. \"I always had some sort of drive and vision of greatness,\" he once said. \"If it was a swimming gala I was at, I pictured myself at the Olympic Games. Leaping through the air at gymnastics, it was the same image I had of myself.\"\n\nIrish people aren't meant to be as confident as this fella, not outwardly anyway. We're meant to be awkward and self-deprecating. McClenaghan, from Newtownards, Co. Down is different. \"I truly believe I can do it,\" McClenaghan says.\n\nIt was his parents who taught him from an early age to have belief.\n\nHe showed that when he left the youth for the senior ranks when he was still a teenager, appreciating that the people he idolised had to cease to become heroes and instead turn into rivals.\n\nMax Whitlock (31), who was third behind Rhys in qualifying, was his big hero.\n\nNow the British star is his big adversary, Whitlock becoming the first man in more than 40 years to retain the Olympic pommel title and he is looking to make further history as the first artistic gymnast to win medals at four Games.\n\nHe isn't the only person blocking Rhys's path to the podium.\n\nJapan's Takaaki Sugino, Ukraine's Oleg Verniaiev, and Kazakstan's Nariman Kurbanov also scored 15 points in qualifying, the pommel's equivalent of the 10-second mark in the 100m sprint. Anything above 10 seconds is considered average in that sport, anything below 15 points considered poor in pommel.\n\nAt the Tokyo Olympics, McClenaghan qualified in joint second and then disaster struck in the final. A fall. A low score. A seventh place finish. A time for reflection.\n\nHe has come back stronger, the five Olympic rings tattooed onto his arm, the idea of becoming a champion imprinted onto his conscience.\n\nHe remembers his breakthrough moment. 2019, finishing third in the world championships, realising he was going to Tokyo.\n\nThat night he phoned home to tell his mother, Tracey, that he had qualified. Neither mother nor son could speak for the tears. \"It was almost like a near-death experience,\" he said.\n\nThose tears stemmed from a devotion to the sport.\n\nWhen he was eight-years-old, he came out of a gymnastics class, and listened to his schedule for the week. Football, swimming, gymnastics. There was a clash. At eight he had to choose between sports. Gymnastics won.\n\nAt times he wondered about the choice. Gym days could be hard; the school playground harder. Kids would abuse him for wearing a leotard. He stood his ground.\n\nBecause nothing in this world has energised him like the feeling he gets when he flies through the air and does a double somersault.\n\nBut this afternoon in Paris - a better feeling may arrive. He has won back to back World and European titles. One prize is missing from his CV ... but today that could change. {"uri": "8256954454", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-03", "time": "05:22:12", "dateTime": "2024-08-03T05:22:12Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-03T05:21:02Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7098039388656616, "url": "https://www.irishexaminer.com/lifestyle/people/arid-41448847.html", "title": "Who is Rhys McClenaghan? Everything you need to know about the Olympic gymnast", "body": "The Co Down man is the first ever Irish gymnast to qualify for two finals at two consecutive Olympic Games, following on from his performance in Tokyo in 2022 - where he was also the first Irish gymnast to qualify for an Olympic final.\n\nHe came first in last week's pommel horse subdivision 3, finishing with a score of 15.200.\n\nMcClenaghan could very well bring home a medal for Team Ireland. Here's everything you need to know about him.\n\nRhys McClenaghan is 25 years old, having celebrated his birthday just days before the Olympics began on July 21.\n\nHe is from Newtownards in Co Down and trained in Rathgael Gymnastics Club in Bangor until his coach was made redundant in 2018.\n\nFollowing this, McClenaghan was forced to train in his back garden before relocating to Dublin. He received funding and accommodation from Gymnastics Ireland and Sport Ireland to train in their institute in Abbotstown.\n\nThe artistic gymnast competes internationally for both Ireland and Northern Ireland. His first major win was in 2016 when he was still technically a minor at the British Artistic Gymnastics Championships. He won a bronze medal, coming third to Olympians Louis Smith and Max Whitlock.\n\nMcClenaghan's speciality is the pommel horse and he is considered to be one of the best pommel workers of his generation.\n\nIn 2018, he won a gold medal on the pommel horse for Northern Ireland at the Commonwealth Games. Months later, he also won the European championship.\n\nThe following year, the Co Down man was the first Irish gymnast to qualify for a world championships final and to take home a medal, following his bronze medal performance on the pommel horse.\n\nMcClenaghan is a double world champion, having won gold on the pommel horse in 2022 and 2023. He is the first Irish artistic gymnast ever to win world championship gold.\n\nLast year, he won a second European title and retained the world title.\n\nAhead of the 2022 Commonwealth Games, McClenaghan was banned from competing for Northern Ireland by the International Gymnastics Foundation, along with two other gymnasts, Eamon Montgomery and Ewan McAteer.\n\nThis was due to the fact that he had previously competed in international competitions for Ireland.\n\nThe foundation called on the men to change their nationality from Irish on their licenses. Alternatively, they suggested that the Commonwealth Games Federation remove the relevant gymnastics competitions from the foundation's calendar.\n\nThen taoiseach Leo Varadkar was among politicians who criticised the decision.\n\nCommonwealth Games NI even accused the foundation of \"completely disregarding\" the Good Friday Agreement in their decision.\n\nEventually, McClenaghan, Montgomery and McClenaghan again balancing his incredibly cool nerve and towering ambition with another dazzling display on the pommel horse - Zen-like in execution, and also devastatingly good. His best routine ever. It needed to be.\n\nHe now stands as the first Irish Olympic medal winner in gymnastics.\n\nRonnie Delany once described destiny as knowing something about yourself that no one else does. Before joining him in that great sporting pantheon as the 11th Irish Olympic champion, McClenaghan had shared his destiny with one other person - his coach Luke Carson.\n\nTogether they drew up a 10-year plan that would have McClenaghan at the peak of his powers by 2024.\n\nSo it came to be. It was just before 6pm local time when the 25-year-old from Newtownards took centre stage for his routine, up fourth of the eight finalists. A flawless 47-second routine later - McClenaghan channelling every ounce of strength and skill and agility built up over that same decade - his score of 15.533 would be enough to seal the gold.\n\nAfter he dismounted McClenaghan raised both arms in triumph and afforded himself a shout of relief, then briefly put his head in his hands as it all started to sink it. Then he embraced Carson.\n\n\"It was hard to make it seem realistic back then, but we saw a path,\" McClenaghan said of their combined goal.\n\n\"Luke clearly saw a path. It's not often I've heard him say, 'I think this kid can be a world champion', but he said it for me. I owe Luke a lot. This is our medal, and I'm proud it's that way.\n\n\"I just think it has been part of this plan, part of what we've been aiming for so many years, since I started training with Luke. You know, that's 10 years ago, and when I started with him, there was a 10-year plan to win an Olympic gold medal.\"\n\nShortly after his routine, McClenaghan was standing on top of the medal podium, Amhr\u00e1n na bhFiann being played out for the first time ever inside an Olympic gymnastics arena. That's when the tears of joy started to fall.\n\n\"It feels like a dream, it's a dream well-earned and I just can't believe it's happened,\" he said later.\n\n\"It always felt like it was going to happen but I wasn't sure when. I didn't know that I'd won when I landed, but I knew that I'd done my job. I knew I went through my most difficult routine that I could do on this day. And that's where the tears and emotions came from, really.\"\n\nNariman Kurbanov from Kazakhstan was up first, the 26-year-old scoring 15.434 and laying down an early marker with his slowly soaring routine which clearly impressed the judges. It would be good enough to win him silver.\n\nThen came Max Whitlock, the 31-year-old British gymnast producing an amazing handstand mid routine, yet his score of 15.000 appeared to slightly disappoint. He would finish in fourth. Whitlock was seeking to become the first gymnast to win three pommel horse Olympic gold medals in a row, adding to those won in Rio and Tokyo.\n\nUp after McClenaghan was Stephen Nedoroscik, the 25-year-old from the US. The 2021 world champion's one-handed twists mid routine impressed the judges too, but his score of 15.300 was not enough to trouble McClenaghan and it left him in third.\n\n\"I literally dreamed that I scored 15.5,\" he said, \"but I never dreamed that I've won, which is weird, but I'm not one to look into dreams like that.\n\n\"There's the tiniest little hesitation during that routine where you can either go for the difficult skill towards the end of the routine, or you don't, and do the routine I've done in qualifications. That would have been a 15.2. So, yeah, I had to suck it up, I had to get it done, put that new skill in, and it worked out because I came away with a personal best score. And, yeah, I can't ask for more than that.\"\n\nMcClenaghan's hopes of making the Olympic podium in Tokyo ended after 10 seconds when he fell chest-first on to the horse and ended up seventh. There were no tears on that occasion, when there might well have been.\n\nIn the press conference afterwards, McClenaghan first took a seat and stared down at his medal.\n\n\"It's better than imagined, and it's got a piece of the Eiffel Tower. And I love Paris so much. It feels like it was just meant to be, on this day. I love seeing that Eiffel Tower. It never gets boring. And now I've got a piece of it hanging around my neck attached to an Olympic gold medal. That's absurd to me.\"\n\nSeven years after announcing his arrival at age 18, beating Whitlock to win gold at the 2018 Commonwealth Games, McClenaghan has added to his eight championship medals (two World Championship gold and one bronze, three European gold, plus Commonwealth Games gold and silver).\n\nHe celebrated on Saturday with family and friends, and his first drink in seven months.\n\n\"All athletes have their own story, but of course I obviously pay close attention to my own, and I'm trying to write that in the happiest fashion I can and that isn't medal-dependant.\n\n\"That's enjoying the journey, enjoying going to competitions around the world, enjoying going training every day. Having gymnastics as my job never gets old, even just saying that, because that in itself is a lifelong dream.\n\n\"It's something I dreamed of when I was in school, literally daydreaming when I was in class about gymnastics routines. The fact that is what I do, that I'm a professional gymnast, is already a win. But then of course the medals are the driving force, that's the pinnacle of our sport, we're always working towards that.\n\n\"I made it very apparent even after Tokyo that I wanted to enjoy that journey to the top of that mountain. {"uri": "8257668555", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-03", "time": "16:59:59", "dateTime": "2024-08-03T16:59:59Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-03T16:56:19Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7058823704719543, "url": "https://www.yahoo.com/news/max-whitlock-knocked-off-final-161625723.html", "title": "Max Whitlock knocked off final Olympic podium by Ireland's Rhys McClenaghan and 'Pommel Horse Guy'", "body": "Max Whitlock was unable to sign off his decorated gymnastics career with a fourth consecutive Olympics medal as Rhys McClenaghan won Ireland's first ever gymnastics gold in the pommel horse final.\n\nWhitlock had confirmed that Paris 2024 would be his final Games after reversing his decision to retire and the 31-year-old was bidding to win a third consecutive gold on the apparatus he had dominated since Rio 2016, to add to his bronze from London 2012.\n\nBut McClenaghan, the two-time world champion from Northern Ireland, won a sensational gold with Kazakhstan's Nariman Kurbanov taking silver and America's Stephan Nedoroscik - aka \"Pommel Horse Guy\" - knocking Whitlock off the podium to win bronze in a high-level final.\n\nMcClenaghan had finished seventh on his Olympics debut in Tokyo three years ago after falling within 10 seconds of his routine, but the 25-year-old had become Ireland's first world champion with back-to-back victories at the World Championships in 2022 and 2023. My coach met with my mum and said 'this kid could be the best in the world on the pommel horse',\" explained McClenaghan.\n\nAs a junior he finished third behind Max Whitlock and Louis Smith at the British Championships.\n\nSmith, who was in Paris to see his friend win the gold, was an inspirational role model for McClenaghan.\n\n\"One of my favourite Olympic moments was watching Louis perform his routine in London. I was at the opening ceremony and I saw all the posters of him all over the city and it felt like the weight of the world was on his shoulders.\n\n\"This felt like my Louis Smith London 2012 moment. I was the reigning world champion and could win Ireland's first ever gymnastics medal.\n\n\"That's why the emotions came out, because I'd done my job under all that pressure.\"\n\nThe Commonwealth Games title and first European win came in 2018 and when the delayed Tokyo Olympics arrived three years later he was among the favourites.\n\nThat day, it didn't happen for McClenaghan.\n\nHowever, since then he has dominated the world with a pair of global and continental titles.\n\n\"It's a crazy journey. All athletes have their own story. Of course, I am paying close attention to my own story but I'm going to write it in the happiest fashion that I can possibly make it.\n\n\"That journey isn't medal-dependant. It is enjoying the journey, enjoying going to competitions around the world, enjoying going to training every day, having gymnastics as my job - it never gets old even just saying that.\n\n\"That in itself is a lifelong dream and something I dreamt of when I was in school.\n\n\"Literally daydreaming in classes about gymnastics routines.\n\n\"So the fact is, that is what I do, that is me, I am a professional gymnast and that is already a win.\n\n\"Yes, the medals are the driving force. That is the pinnacle of our sport and I'm always working towards that, but I made it very apparent after Tokyo that I wanted to enjoy that journey to the top of that mountain.\n\n\"Even if I didn't walk away with a medal today I would have a lifetime of experience, a lifetime of wins and losses, but today was a win.\"\n\nFor the first time, there were three current world champions from Northern Ireland competing at an Olympic Games.\n\nAll three - McClenaghan, Hannah Scott and Daniel Wiffen - have become Olympic champions in the space of just five days for what has arguably been the greatest fell from the apparatus, when he was favoured to win gold at the Tokyo Olympics.\n\nHe told Sky News that he slept last night with the medal on his bedside table.\n\nHe said: \"It's so heavy and so McClenaghan there.\n\n\"The upset in Tokyo, straight after that Olympic Games final where I fell I said I am going to turn this disappointment into motivation and come back better, stronger than ever and that is exactly what I done.\n\n\"I am a two-time world champion, now an Olympic champion and that is the redemption I wanted.\"\n\nMcClenaghan was emotional following his success on Saturday.\n\n\"Everything I've done since I started gymnastics was demonstrated in that 46-second routine that I showed yesterday.\n\n\"It was all of the emotions, all of them under the sun were being expressed that day and you could see I was switching between hysterically laughing, hysterically crying, it was just overwhelming.\"\n\nThe gymnast from Newtownards also paid tribute to his parents It is not just that four years or three years of work, it is a lifetime of work.\n\n\"Everything I've done since I started gymnastics was demonstrated in that 46-second routine that I showed yesterday.\n\n\"It was all of the emotions, all of them under the sun were being expressed that day and you could see I was switching between hysterically laughing, hysterically crying, it was just overwhelming.\"\n\nThe gymnast from Newtownards also paid tribute to his parents, Danny and Tracy, who were in Paris to watch his gold medal victory.\n\nHe said: \"I only started getting funding for this sport when I turned 18 years old.\n\n\"I started gymnastics at six years old, that gap between there, that is all my parents, that is them driving me to and from the gym, paying my gymnastics Paris, back in his home town of Newtownards a crowd of fans waited with bated breath until his perfectly executed dismount.\n\nThere were cheers at Origin Gymnastics, where McClenaghan trains, but more nerves as the remainder of the gymnasts in the event final completed their routines and the scores were complete.\n\nOrigin senior coach Conor McGovern confirmed the gold to cheers of approval from those gathered waving banners of support.\n\nMeanwhile political leaders took to social media to celebrate.\n\nStormont First Minister Michelle O'Neill posted on X: \"Rhys McClenaghan makes history as the first ever gymnast from our island to win gold at the Olympics!\n\n\"What an incredible career that is going from strength to strength, congratulations Rhys!\"\n\nDeputy First Minister There's a real art to that. He has treated every session like it was an Olympic Games final. And the point of that is that when it comes to the Olympic Games final, that it's just another training session.\n\n\"Don't get overwhelmed by the 12 and a half thousand people and the reality that it's the highest standard pommel final of all time and that there's probably going to be a few world records set and you're going to have to top that.\n\n\"Obviously those are realities but you can't control a lot of those things, so what I tried to do is say to him just to focus on what we can control. He knew that and that's something that he's really mastered, and that's why he is now an Olympic champion.\"\n\nBut where does that laser focus come from? I think it's what sets me apart from many other athletes.\n\n\"You just hear of some people having bad days and saying they'll have a better day tomorrow. I always felt like it was going to happen, I just wasn't sure when.'\n\nMcClenaghan arrived at the eight-man final as favourite after heading qualifying and with his two world titles.\n\nBut he also had to contend with heaps of pressure after falling off the apparatus at the Covid-delayed Tokyo Games three years ago to trail in seventh.\n\nHe wasn't about to let this second shot at writing his name in Irish sporting folklore go awry though.\n\nWith a score of 15.533 he wept tears of joy on the top of the podium as he listened to 'Amhran na bhFiann' - the Irish national anthem - with green, white and orange tricolours being waved proudly from the stands by his legion of supporters at the Bercy Arena.\n\nKurbanov had been the first to perform and he set a high bar with a score of 15.433.\n\nNext up was Whitlock, seeking an unprecedented fourth medal on the same apparatus after golds at the last two Games in Tokyo and Rio, and bronze at London 2012.\n\nAnd the 31-year-old's slick Olympic swansong was enough to leave him sitting third behind McClenaghan and Kurbanov until Nedoroscik took his place on the podium by 0.100pts.\n\nNedoroscik is the US gymnastics team's one-trick pony - the pommel horse his sole speciality.\n\nHis father bought him an old one from the 1980s at auction when he was a youngster and from that moment he was hooked.\n\n'When you only do one event you don't get to test the others. You just go out there and swing. But I've been doing this for eight years now as a specialist, so really tested the waters. I feel amazing,' said the man from Massachusetts.\n\nHe had posted the same score as McClenaghan in qualifying, meaning Ireland's third gold medallist of the 2024 Games had a tense wait to see if his American title rival would gatecrash his party.\n\nHe said he purposely hadn't watched those performing after him, but that didn't stop the nerves.\n\n'I was nervous. I'm nervous in any competition. I'm nervous in every training session. I feel like the pressure I put on my shoulders pushes me every day. That's what resulted in this.'\n\nAnd what does the immediate future hold in store for the new prince of the pommel horse?\n\n'I want to see my friends and family. Olympic gold his crowning glory.\n\nWatching the pommel horse final was a gripping 40 minutes or so.\n\nA week ago, Stephen Nedoroscik's potential as a gold medallist was flagged up by RT\u00c9 commentator Colm Murray; in qualifying both he and McClenaghan posted the same score. Max Whitlock, in this his swansong event, could not be discounted. And then there was Nariman Kurbanov from Kazakhstan.\n\nAnd it was the latter who set the target in the final, though to me and my untutored eye, Max Whitlock's routine looked impressive, that was until he lacked a variety in his movements towards the end. \"You only use so many elements (types of movement), you then have to change. If not, the general person in the crowd would get bored,\" that was Colm Murray's summation of the scoring in gymnastics. I did take notes on what he had to say, you know!\n\nAnd then Rhys took to the horse, exactly three years on from when his maiden Olympic Games ended as a result of a minor slip in the final. European and World titles would follow, catapulting him as the man to beat.\n\nThe sizeable Irish crowd in the Bercy Arena gave Rhys a huge welcome, cheered his every turn and twist as went through his routine. The cheering grew louder, yet one feared that his hand might slip off the edge, but that's part of the thrill. The pommel horse, like all the other artistic gymnastic disciplines, is so much more impressive in the flesh. Simone Biles, a half an hour earlier, pounding on to that vault and adding to her medal haul, is better watched live.\n\nMcCleneghan then landed on the floor with a kind of thump. The packed arena were appreciative of his efforts. Kurbanov's score was 15.434, an impressive mark. And then we all looked up at that screen in the middle; target score on the left and then a graphic, after what felt like an eternity, would reveal 15.533 as McClenaghan's score. Our man was in front.\n\nA short break in the action. Over to you Stephen Nedoroscik. The United States following in the crowd was substantial and Nedoroscik was performing to a stirring soundtrack. His score of 15.300 was met by huge cheers from the Irish contingent; Nedoroscik and McCleneghan would hug each other. They're good friends, but the man from Newtownards, even with three participants still to perform, had gold in the bag.\n\nThe 25-year-old's climb to the top of the mountain was complete. Of course he will want to stay there. What an amazing final to be a part of. I said to Stephren that this is the best pommel horse final that has ever happened in the history of men's gymnastics. To be a part of that is absolutely incredible.\"\n\nMcClenaghan lamented the fact that he didn't get into the 9s in the execution. All gymnasts want that 'perfect 10' but that chase of perfection, a chase that isn't always attainable, is what drives those at the top forward.\n\nFor McClenaghan, a weight has now been lifted off him. His trajectory, after World and European success, had to show an Olympic gold on some point of the graph. He knew that Nedoroscik could do a huge difficulty and pull it off when it mattered, but even the American knew that the Newtownards man had set a mark that was beyond his reach.\n\nAnd so a third gold medal for Ireland at these Games, in a sport we never we thought we could or should be medalling in.\n\nFor McClenaghan, he has completed the grand slam. Three years since that slip in Tokyo. \"It's been a crazy journey and I'm trying to write in the happiest fashion I can possibly make it,\" he added when summing up the time since.\n\n\"I want to enjoy the journey, going to competitions around the world, going training. Gymnastics is my job and it's something I dreamed of when I was in school. I was daydreaming in class about gymnastic routines. Being a professional gymnast is already a win.\"\n\nAnd now he scaled the heights, with golden medals to cement his status in the sport.\n\nThe journey continues. {"uri": "8257765041", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-03", "time": "18:44:44", "dateTime": "2024-08-03T18:44:44Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-03T18:43:57Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5921568870544434, "url": "https://www.irishnews.com/sport/it-shows-i-have-balls-anyway-after-the-nightmare-of-tokyo-mcclenaghan-climbs-back-on-the-horse-to-achieve-olympic-dream-Y5JD4AI4YRETROSZ4ONQEPKQD4/", "title": "'It shows I have balls anyway': After the nightmare of Tokyo, McClenaghan climbs back on the horse to achieve Olympic dream", "body": "Newtownards gymnast takes gold in Paris - three years after costly slip in Tokyo\n\nAFTER the nightmare of Tokyo, this was the moment Rhys McClenaghan has been dreaming of.\n\nEver since coach Luke Carson sat down with a 15-year-old McClenaghan and his parents, Danny and Tracey, this was the end goal. All he asked of them all was they believed as much as he did.\n\n\"If couldn't get them on board, it just wasn't possible,\" said Carson, \"because otherwise it's just a crazy guy saying things.\"\n\nThree years on from Tokyo, when McClenaghan's Olympic dreams went up in smoke after a slip of the fingers inside 10 seconds, it appears destined that their decade-long plan would be realised in Paris.\n\nThis time, there would be no mistakes, and no regrets. Whether he won gold, medalled or came home with nothing at all, so be it. If he gave his best possible performance and was beaten by the better man, he could live with that.\n\nBut, from the moment McClenaghan walked into a packed Bercy Arena, he was tuned into another world, determined not to absorb any of the emotion still flying around moments after US superstar Simone Biles had claimed a third gold of the Games.\n\nFourth on the pommel, he either looked the other way or turned his back on opponents' routines; not looking at them, not looking at their score. This was about him and no-one else.\n\nNariman Kurbanov from Kazakhstan was first up, hitting an impressive 15.434. It was a standard defending champion Max Whitlock was unable to reach as his dreams of hanging on to gold disappeared in that instant.\n\nWhen McClenaghan's turn came, a hush fell upon the arena. Even with all he has achieved in the time between, the two World golds in particular, you wondered was there anything left of Tokyo that might creep in when the pressure reached its peak.\n\nThere wasn't. He promised to raise his game again from last week's heat, and he did so in some style to he score 15.533 (difficulty 6.600, execution 8.933), up from 15.200 in qualifying.\n\nThe second his feet touched the matt upon dismount, it all came out; a huge roar lost in the applause of the crowd as he grabbed the lead at the halfway mark, tears already flowing by the time he reached his chair.\n\n\"No matter what happened after that, I had done my job...\"\n\nThe rest was pure tension and drama it what is now considered the highest quality pommel horse final of all time. American Stephen Nedoroscik produced a magical routine and, Irish stomachs churning as the familiar sound of a pulse filled the arena during the interminable wait for a score to materialise.\n\nAnd then it came - 15.300. One of the men expected to give McClenaghan the biggest run for his money has just fallen short. The dream was now in sight as, one by one, each challenger failed to hit his incredible height.\n\n\"I literally dreamed it,\" he beamed, \"I literally dreamed that I scored a 15.5, but I never dreamed that I won, which is weird.\n\n\"I'm not one to look into dreams like that, I just think it has been part of this plan, part of what we've been aiming for for so many years since I started training with Luke. That's 10 years ago, and when I started with him, there was a 10-year plan to win an Olympic gold medal, and here it is today.\"\n\nTokyo had taken a toll, there's no question. McClenaghan tried to play it down after his heat, having a nip at journalists for referring back to that fateful day three summers ago.\n\n\"I've done a lot of competitions that you guys didn't even watch in between that...\"\n\nBut Carson described that Olympic experience as \"traumatic\" for gymnast and coach. To come back to the same stage, with the huge weight of expectation that winning world titles brings, requires incredible self-belief, and maybe a little bit more.\n\n\"It shows I've got balls anyway.\n\n\"There was certainly added pressure today, but I put pressure on myself every single day in training and there was one session - Tuesday this week - I split my legs on the upgraded skill that I put in the routine and I was raging with myself. I was angry.\n\n\"It felt like it was a competition but at the same time when I do clean routines, I get as pumped as I feel during competitions. And that comes from that pressure I put on myself - I get nervous in training sessions and I feel like because of that respect that I give each training session, that's why I can perform in competition environments like today.\" Last week, McClenaghan said he was sitting on the top of the mountain, enjoying the view. If that was the case, then his head must now surely be in the clouds, though pizza and a few beers were the only things on his agenda as Saturday evening turned into night.\n\nYet, even in the most manic of moments, he speaks with the composure and clarity of a little Buddha.\n\nBecause this gold medal, for as much as it represented the high point of a breathtaking personal journey, is also a platform to promote the sport he loves, and to show the next generation that anything is possible.\n\nEven if, at some points, there is no choice but to get back on the horse.\n\n\"The view is really, really nice right now. That view consists of seeing my younger self having these dreams, seeing me enjoying the journey, seeing a bunch of kids inspired by this Olympic gold medal.\n\n\"Even if I didn't come away with that, I still would've had that goal of trying to inspire as many as possible. I want to do as much for this sport as I can.\n\n\"After this medal I want to bring it to hopefully every club in Ireland, I want to be really active in inspiring that younger generation, because this is a responsibility on my shoulders.\n\n\"I could lock my door and stay away, not talk to anybody for the next year or two, but I'm not going to do that, I'm going to be out, I'm going to be inspiring {"uri": "8257825506", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-03", "time": "20:01:53", "dateTime": "2024-08-03T20:01:53Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-03T20:00:12Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5921568870544434, "url": "https://www.thesun.ie/sport/13544395/rhys-mcclenaghan-2024-olympic-games-paris/", "title": "I love Paris so much -McClenaghan say Olympic gold in France was meant to be", "body": "Three years ago, McClenaghan's finger got caught in one of the bars on the pommel horse, ending his medal hopes as he finished seventh\n\nIN A CITY which has been known to behead kings, Rhys McClenaghan is happy to stick with his existing nickname as The Prince of Pommel.\n\nBut there was no doubt that Newtownards man was master of all he surveyed in the Bercy Arena.\n\nMainly, he was just gazing at the gold medal hanging around his neck.\n\nHe said: \"I love Paris so much, it feels like it was just meant to be, this day.\n\n\"I love Paris, I love seeing that Eiffel Tower, it never gets boring, now I've got a piece of it hanging around my neck, attached to an Olympic gold medal. That's absurd to me.\"\n\nThree years ago, McClenaghan's finger got caught in one of the bars on the pommel horse, ending his medal hopes as he finished seventh.\n\nStraight away, the then 22-year-old articulated his ambition to bounce back by taking gold in Paris. He realised that in style.\n\nHe said: \"All athletes have their own story but of course I obviously pay close attention to my own story.\n\n\"But I'm trying to write that in the happiest fashion I can possibly make it and that isn't medal-dependant.\n\n\"That is enjoying the journey, enjoying going to competitions around the world, enjoying going training every day, having gymnastics as my job never gets old, even just saying that, because that in itself is a life-long dream.\n\n\"That's something I dreamed of when I was in school, literally day-dreaming when I was in class about gymnastics routines.\n\n\"The fact that is what I do, that I'm a professional gymnast, is already a win.\n\n\"But then of course the medals are the driving force, that's the pinnacle of our sport, we're always working towards that.\n\n\"I made it very apparent even after Tokyo that I wanted to enjoy that journey to the top of that mountain.\"\n\nFourth up in the eight-man final, he posted a score of 15.333. He insisted that he did not know straight away that he had done enough to win gold but he had.\n\nHe knocked Kazakhstan's Nariman Kurbanov off top spot with Stephen Nedoroscik of the USA the only one of the subsequent competitors to get a score above 15 with his 15.200 enough for bronze.\n\nMcClenaghan said: \"I didn't know that I'd won as soon as I landed. But I knew that I had done my job.\n\n\"I'd done the most difficult routine I could do on this day and that's where the tears and the emotions came from really.\n\n\"Either of these guys can beat that score but what an amazing final to be a part of. This was the best pommel horse final that has ever happened in my eyes, ever, in the history of men's gymnastics.\n\n\"To be a part of that is incredible and any one of those eight finalists could have taken gold today.\"\n\nMcClenaghan did so by attempting and executing his most difficult routine yet.\n\nHe said: \"It shows I've got balls anyway. There's a tiniest little hesitation during that routine where you can either go for the difficult skill towards the end of the routine or you don't.\n\n\"That routine I did in qualifications that would've been a 15.2 I would've scored.\n\n\"So, I had to suck it up, get it done, put that new skill in and it worked out because I came away with a personal best score. I can't ask for more than that.\n\n\"But there's a couple of height circles that I would have liked to stretch out a bit more. I'm glad I glued those legs together towards the end of the routine.\n\n\"That was always a goal and that's something that I've been susceptible to in the past - it's like a nervous thing, if you ever see a pommel horse worker split their legs, it's like they're nervous as if they felt something's off a little bit.\n\n\"But I want to feel even if something is off that I can glue those legs together and I feel like I done that today.\n\n\"And then of course just to have the balls to do that added skill, not backing out of it. I could have just played it safe, done the qualification routine like normal.\n\n\"But instead we went out there and did the highest difficulty I had ever put out in an international competition. I risked it all and came away with the biscuit.\"\n\nIt represent the realisation of a 10-year plan with Luke Carson who, he said, turned him from a mediocre gymnast.\n\nHe said: \"Luke clearly saw a path. It's not often I've heard him say 'I think this kid can be a world champion', but he said it for me.\n\n\"So he clearly saw a path that I could take, we took that path, I followed Luke on every programme he done, questioned him very little, although of course it's healthy to question your coach every now and then.\n\n\"I owe Luke a lot, this is our medal and I'm proud it's that way.\"\n\nBut he might hang onto this medal himself having given one of his World Championships golds to Carson for his Origin Gymnastics gym to inspire the next generation.\n\nAnd he knows from personal experience how a Games can do that for a youngster.\n\nMcClenaghan said: \"I just saw Louis Smith there in the media zone and one of my favourite Olympic moments, maybe my favourite, was when he performed his routine at the London 2012 Olympic Games.\n\n\"I went to and watched the Opening Ceremony and saw posters of Lewis Smith everywhere.\n\n\"It seemed like the weight of the world was on his shoulders and it felt like this was my Louis Smith London 2012 moment coming in here.\n\n\"I'm the double world champion coming into this, Ireland knew this could be our first ever Olympic gymnastics medal and I delivered under that pressure.\n\n\"That was the emotion that you were seeing there that I had done my job regardless of that pressure I was under.\n\n\"I talked to him in the media zone. It's mad seeing him standing right there in my Olympic moment, when his Olympic moment was my inspiration for my entire career.\n\n\"That right there is a working example of that inspiration working and that trickledown effect.\n\n\"There might be a kid in the crowd there that is saying, 'Jesus that was amazing.' That was so good, I want to do that.'\n\n\"That might stick in their brain -- into whatever sport they do, whatever job they do, career path they do. And that might inspire them to be the best they can possibly be.\n\n\"That shows the power of these Olympic Games.\n\n\"I could lock my door and stay away, not talk to anybody for the next year or two, but I'm not going to do that.\n\n\"I'm going to be out, I'm going to be inspiring hopefully, and I want as many kids as possible to touch this medal and see that it's a reality as much as {"uri": "8258042679", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-04", "time": "02:04:04", "dateTime": "2024-08-04T02:04:04Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-04T01:35:44Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.5882353186607361, "url": "https://www.independent.ie/sport/olympics/rhys-mccleneghans-confidence-desire-and-energy-to-beat-setbacks-have-carried-him-to-gold/a60401868.html", "title": "Rhys McCleneghan's confidence, desire and energy to beat setbacks have carried him to gold", "body": "Rhys McClenaghan's Olympic gold medal may contain a fragment of the Eiffel Tower but it was forged in Newtownards, under the shadow of Scrabo Tower.\n\n\"I was doing gymnastics before I even knew what gymnastics were,\" he laughed.\n\n\"As soon as I could walk I was jumping on stuff and climbing up things. It wasn't long before I was doing handstands and blackflips in the back garden.\"\n\nBorn to swimming coach Tracy and keen sportsman Danny, young Rhys was a bundle of energy from day one.\n\nLike most hyperactive children he tried his hand at all sports. He played rugby at school, football for his local amateur club Ards Rangers and he swam competitively for Ards Swimming Club - a club with a proud history of producing Olympians and Paralympians.\n\nBut Rhys' pathway to the Games came via another route.\n\n\"I think my parents took me to gymnastics lessons just so I could burn off some energy without breaking the furniture.\n\n\"A gymnastics centre is a magical place for a kid. There's a big foam put, there's bars to swing on, there's a trampoline to bounce on and a spring-loaded floor. What more do you want?\"\n\nRhys wanted glory. Olympic glory. Right from his mid-teens he told anyone that would listen that his aim was to become 'the best in the world.' To be 'undeniable.'\n\nHis breakthrough came as a fifth former at Regent House in 2016 when - aged just 16 - he finished third on the pommel horse at the British Championships, just behind Max Whitlock and Louis Smith, two established Olympians preparing for the Rio Games.\n\nHe defeated Whitlock for the first time on the Gold Coast just two years later, when a sublime routine secured a gold medal at the Commonwealth Games. At 18, he was already a household name in Northern Ireland.\n\nAnd when he returned home from Australia, he was paraded through Newtownards on an open to bus before greeted hundreds of fans from a specially erected stage in Conway Square.\n\nAs if he wasn't popular enough in his home town, when journalists referred to him as 'Bangor's Rhys McClenaghan', he took to Twitter to put them right.\n\nThere is an unfair assumption in some parts that Rhys might be disliked in his hometown because he represents Ireland. And while you'll always find the odd idiot in the comments section, Rhys has always been held in great affection by the people of Newtownards.\n\n\"I've seen comments on social media,\" McClenaghan told the Irish Independent. \"But they're not real people saying it to your face. Not one person has said one negative thing to my face around what country I should represent.\"\n\nThat connection with his hometown remains as strong as ever. Earlier this year, he and his coach Luke Carson opened their gym - Origin Gymnastics - in the town's Blair Mayne Complex.\n\n\"I want to see the younger generation come through and follow in my footsteps, surpassing my achievements. I just want to see people enjoying the sport of gymnastics.\"\n\nHe is also club ambassador for Ards FC - a role he doesn't take lightly. McClenaghan has supported the homeless club's campaign for a new ground, and delivered an inspirational speech to the first team during the heat of a relegation battle.\n\nInspired by athletes like Michael Jordan, artists like Kendrick Lamar and authors like Jessie Barr, McClenaghan is a deep-thinker. Don't be surprised if works in sports psychology once he dismounts from the pommel horse for the last time.\n\nAfter all, he's had to deal with his fair share of setbacks. In 2018, Rathgael Gymnastic Club - the Bangor club where he had trained for a decade - cut their coaching programme in an acrimonious dispute that made front page headlines.\n\nAfter a stint practicing on his own pommel horse in his back garden, McClenaghan was forced to relocate to Dublin and train at the National Training Centre, with coach Luke Carson making the long commute from Lisburn.\n\nHe also had to fight tooth and nail for his right to defend his Commonwealth title at Birmingham 2022, after a bizarre decision - eventually reversed after huge backlash - barred the Northern Ireland team from competing at the Games.\n\nAnd then there was Tokyo. That agonising finger slip in the Olympic final that cost him a real tilt at Max Whitlock in the medals.\n\nBut with his incredible mental strength and outrageous ability, McClenaghan has overcome all those setbacks to climb to the top of the Olympic mountain.
