McClenaghan has released his latest YouTube video. He filmed the final clip in his room on Friday night. It was a full outline of his emotional wellbeing. One bed, a bare chest, his intense stare made up the frame. Pared back and raw.\n\nConsider that for a moment. Several Irish athletes step away from social media entirely before the Olympics, entrusting it to a handler for the duration of the games. Others do whatever they can to take their mind off the competition. American bronze medallist Stephen Nedoroscik told the press conference he has taken to solving rubik's cube to keep him occupied.\n\nMcClenaghan is different. We know this because he exposes it, weekly on his own channel. Given that his qualifier took place seven days ago with no media duties in between, it means the only insight into his mental state is through that lens. What an insight it is, too.\n\n\"I am just sitting on my bed editing this video,\" explained. \"I am only realising I haven't really gathered too much footage. I guess my mind has been on other things. It is currently the night before my Olympic Games final. I guess this section can just be an honest piece to camera for you guys.\n\n\"I feel like I have already accomplished what I set out to do. I enjoyed every second of this Olympic Games experience, I will hopefully enjoy every second of this final tomorrow. It really was the contrast I needed to see in comparison to the Tokyo Games.\"\n\nThree years ago in Tokyo, McClenaghan fell off the pommel and finished seventh. The road from there to here has been documented thoroughly. In one sense, it should be expected that the 25-year-old is at the forefront of a new digital wave. He is one of several Irish athletes charting their story online. The social media age has expanded and overrun all kinds of traditional borders in the process.\n\nSome of that self-generated content is good, plenty isn't. This week McClenaghan brought the camera around the Olympic village and through the security checks. He spoke openly about the absence of the anxious energy that haunted him in Tokyo. Last time he didn't take advantage of the merchandise shop to stock up on gifts for friends and family. He didn't savour the glow around the star.\n\nCome along to the podium on training day. His first visit to the arena, his use of competition equipment, his ability to transform his mindset to the point of mental pressure that stimulates qualification.\n\nThis sport is defined by such mental fortitude. As Kazakhstani Nariman Kurbanov took to the floor, McClenaghan sat with his head bowed. His feet scarcely touched the ground, dangling gently beneath him. His position for rival Max Whitlock's subsequent performance was similar. He rose to stretch and turned his back to Loran de Munck. Then the Dutch man fell.\n\nHow would that affect him? There is another moment for his YouTube for this moment. During his practice round in Paris, McClenaghan zoomed his camera in on the pommel horse. Right up close. Blood stains spattered its veneer. He didn't do it out of disgust. This was a testament to his code, a representation of the hard work required to make it to the top. Falling is a battle scar. Show them with pride.\n\nRegardless of how Saturday went, the Newtownards man was heading home content. This isn't hindsight stuff spewed to absolve the pain of a defeat. He told us this the night before.\n\n\"It was just a completely different experience. There are a lot of things going through my mind right now, but the one at the forefront of it is I am incredibly proud of myself and all of the work and dedication and sacrifice I have done to get here.\n\n\"That is why it is a celebration just to be in this Olympic Games final. I know I am ready, so let's show up with a smile on my face and have the best time.\"\n\nThe goal. That is how he wanted to go out. How did it go? He stood on top of the podium and cradled the gold medal in both hands. As the final notes of Amhr\u00e1n na bhFiann sang, McClenaghan looked straight across from him at a green mass celebrating and his lips began to twitch. {"uri": "8258562582", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-08-04", "time": "11:32:42", "dateTime": "2024-08-04T11:32:42Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-08-04T11:30:48Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.4941176474094391, "url": "https://www.irishnews.com/sport/leaps-of-faith-tokyo-trauma-and-finding-calm-in-the-middle-of-the-storm-why-olympic-gold-was-the-realisation-of-a-dream-for-more-than-mcclenaghan-BSGYAV5D75BV7HRSNIMZ4LP6B4/", "title": "Leaps of faith, Tokyo trauma and finding calm in the middle of the storm - why Olympic gold was the realisation of a dream for more than McClenaghan", "body": "Coach Luke Carson has been there since the beginning, dreaming about Paris 2024 moment\n\nLUKE Carson was lost in a daze as he paced the corridors around the basement of the Bercy Arena.\n\nThree floors up, slack-jawed spectators were still in deep discussion about the highest quality pommel horse final of all time - and the fact that Ireland's Rhys McClenaghan, courtesy of a breathtaking routine, had finished top of the pile.\n\nOver on the other side of the same bottom floor, the new Olympic champion was just about to walk into the press conference room and take his place between American Stephen Nedoroscik (silver) and Kazakhstan's Nariman Kurbanov (bronze).\n\nCoach Carson, with McClenaghan since the very beginning, shook his head and wandered. He didn't look at his phone, he wasn't talking to anybody else. For those moments it was just him, alone with his thoughts, trying to make sense of how the most crazy, ambitious of dreams sold 10 years earlier had somehow been realised.\n\n\"What I kept thinking last night, going to sleep, is that this has been such a personal journey.\n\n\"Like, everything that we've done from the very moment that me and Rhys linked up to this moment, I wrote down in 2014, and within it was every major title.\n\n\"I showed him, I showed his parents and I told him, and what I needed to do was to make them believe that it could happen. Because if I couldn't get them on board, it just wasn't possible... otherwise it's just this crazy guy saying things.\n\n\"But I saw a spark in him so young and I believed it - I truly, honestly believed it. And there was such a vulnerability in believing something that much because it's not guaranteed, obviously. Anything can happen, the margins of error are so small.\n\n\"But I truly, honestly believed with every fibre in my body, to the point where I visualised this so many times, it is like a dream I've had 30,000 times already, that it just felt like a jigsaw piece.\n\n\"It just went together perfectly.\"\n\nFor a 15-year-old to shun the typical trappings of teenage life, in exchange for a one in a million shot at pulling off something remarkable, requires a leap of faith beyond all comprehension.\n\nBut McClenaghan and Carson are, and always have been, so much more than gymnast and coach.\n\n\"It's hard to make it seem realistic back then but we saw a path - Luke clearly saw a path,\" said McClenaghan.\n\n\"It's not often I've heard him say 'I think this kid can be a world champion', but he said it for me... I owe Luke a lot. This is our medal and I'm proud it's that way.\"\n\nFrom July 15, the day they swapped Ireland for Paris, Carson was convinced that what unfolded on Saturday evening was already written. He had seen it day in, day out in training, far from crowded arenas and media glare.\n\nBut Tokyo was always there too. Because, coming into those Olympic Games three years ago, McClenaghan is in even more electrifying form on the pommel. His success rate was 100 per cent.\n\n\"That's unheard of,\" said Carson, \"he was absolutely on fire.\"\n\nAnd then, inside 10 seconds, it was over. The click of fingers, just like, what was supposed to be a dream turned into a nightmare.\n\nManaging the pressure of the situation, controlling only what affected him, those were the key factors in laying those demons to rest on Saturday.\n\n\"That's the maturity of him as an athlete... Tokyo taught him that. Tokyo taught him a hard lesson.\n\n\"You know, to be perfectly honest, Tokyo, from both of our point of views, was really traumatic. Like, I felt like a part of my soul died that day.\n\n\"But unfortunately, sometimes you need to make those mistakes of being almost intimidated by the big scores that have come out, and then the psychology switches slightly, then it's not just about me and my routine, it's 'I've got to beat this subjective score, it's out of my control'.\n\n\"And so from Tokyo to now has been an open, honest discussion that we can't control scores; it's completely out of our control, there's zero point in worrying about it or thinking about it. What you can control is your gymnastics and how you frame it.\n\n\"So what I said to him yesterday during line up was today would be a battle of the wits. All the endurance is done, all the hard work is done, but today is the battle of the wits.\n\n\"You have to be able to block all that out because none of it matters. And he did that today. What he did today is what he's done in every day in training for the last year, and I promise you I'm not joking.\n\n\"He has treated every session like it was an Olympic Games final, and the point of that is that when it comes to the Olympic Games final that it's just another training session.\n\n\"He knew that and that's something that he's really mastered, and that's why he is now an Olympic Sinead O'Carroll Reports from Paris\n\nTHERE ARE SIX apparatus in men's gymnastics and Rhys McClenaghan, Newtownards pommel horse specialist, is the world's undeniable best on one of them.\n\nHowever, Dozens of gymnasts still had to perform after him and he was finally edged out of the last, eighth qualifying position seven hours later.\n\n\"Achin,\" he tweeted from his hotel room.\n\nHe was World champion three months later and returned to Ireland that week and explained how he had done it. One aspect of his routine was catching him out. He wasn't quite nailing it and the risk/reward ratio demanded that it be dropped. So it was dropped.\n\nThat World title was defended 12 months later. He added two European titles with it, but that tricky routine couldn't be discarded forever, To go where he really wanted to go, to be an Olympic champion, he had to return to it and crack it.\n\nHe did that in Paris.\n\n\"So the routine I went back to, the structure is the same, but pretty much the skill that was giving me issues before that World Championships had to go back into the routine if we were going to bump the difficulty up even more.\n\n\"So I performed the highest difficulty I've ever performed in that routine [on Saturday] and it's what won me that title,\" he explained. \"You've just got to risk it for that top spot. That's how high the standard was.\" There came a point in the routine where two paths presented themselves.\n\nTake the easier one, the one he nailed in qualifying, and it would have given him a score of 15.200. But Nariman Kurbanov had already registered a 15.433. The other path was the only path. It delivered his best ever competitive score and an Olympic gold medal.\n\nHis difficulty rating in Tokyo was 6.4. Paris was 6.6. That's the difference between gold and silver.\n\n\"It shows I've got balls anyway,\" he laughed.\n\nJustin Spring, a bronze medallist with the USA in the All-Around in 2008, went viral online with his rising levels of incredulity as the routine developed.\n\n\"I thought he was going to take off on that one. That was perfect.\" \"Wow, that was so intricate.\" \"I really don't know what you take off for that routine.\" \"He did it. Oh man, he did it!\" Eurosport's commentator said she would have believed McClenaghan's legs had been glued together had she not seen him walk. Louis Smith, who McClenaghan had watched win medals in London in 2012 when barely a teenager, labelled it the greatest Pommel display he had ever seen.\n\nMcClenaghan got to meet Smith in the mixed zone afterwards. It was \"mad,\" he said, to meet the Englishman at that very moment when it had been Smith who had served as the inspiration for his entire career. It all added to the perfect day.\n\nHe looked down more than once at the gold medal hanging around his neck and marvelled at how there was an actual piece of the Eiffel Tower embedded in it, and about how much he loves the city of Paris. It all felt like the pieces had fallen into place.\n\nThere were yet more eloquent words about the mental health journey he has been on, how he has limited his social media, found every scintilla of joy in the journey and daily life, and how he would have still been fulfilled had the outcome been different at the weekend.\n\nWhat he didn't say was that it had been in that very same Bercy Arena, in the autumn of last year, where he had experienced what he later termed was his first panic attack, just weeks before he defended his World title in Antwerp.\n\nSo, yeah, Tokyo to Paris has a lot to sell it on the big screen.
